# Security review checklist

Every source file in the repo (git-tracked, excludes node_modules and build output like dist/ and /*/lib/). Work through the list one file at a time
using the process below. Only tick a file off once the whole process has been completed for it and the test suite passes.

Generated: 2026-07-13. Total files: 3528

## How to check a file

Follow this loop for each file. Don't batch several files together: finish one, tick it, move to the next. See TESTING.md for the testing conventions
(Vitest, `runStory`, `captureRequester`, mock rules).

### 1. Understand the file

- Read the whole file, not just the exports. Note every function it declares and what each is responsible for.
- Check who calls it (grep for the export names) so you know what inputs can actually reach it, especially whether any input is user-controlled
  (request params, file paths, KVS keys, headers, tokens).

### 2. Make sure tests exist

- Look for the sibling `<name>.test.ts` file. Tests live next to the source they cover.
- For every function in the file, confirm there is at least one test for the happy path and one for each meaningful failure path (bad input, thrown
  errors, empty results).
- Write any tests that are missing. Stories use `runStory` with a full mock map; requester functions use `captureRequester`. Every action must be
  mocked, so missing coverage will show itself quickly.
- Run the tests and make sure they pass before you change anything. If they fail on a clean checkout, fix the tests (or the code, if the test is
  exposing a real bug) before moving on. You need a green baseline, otherwise you can't tell whether your later changes broke something.

### 3. Review the code

Go through the file looking for three kinds of problems:

- **Bugs**: wrong logic, unhandled error paths, off-by-one, bad async handling (missing `await`, unhandled rejections), incorrect fallbacks, dead code
  that hides intent.
- **Security flaws**, in particular:
  - Injection: anything interpolated into a query, shell command, file path, or URL. Watch for path traversal on file actions and unescaped values in
    Neo4j/graph queries.
  - AuthN/AuthZ: endpoints or actions that skip auth checks, trust a client-supplied identity, or leak another tenant's data (check the `scope` param
    on File/KVS actions).
  - Secrets: hardcoded credentials, secrets or tokens written to logs, secrets in error messages.
  - Overly broad permissions: IAM wildcards, account-scoped ARNs, presigned URLs with long expiry or wrong scope.
  - Unsafe deserialization or `eval`-like patterns, prototype pollution on merged objects.
  - Missing input validation on anything that crosses a trust boundary.
- **Error handling**: processors must map every AWS error name to a first-class entry in the action's own `*ErrorTypeEnum`. A raw `[errorName]` in a
  GenericError means the map is incomplete: add it.

### 4. Fix what you find

- Fix bugs and security issues directly. Keep the fix minimal and in the style of the surrounding code.
- Add a test that reproduces each issue before (or alongside) the fix, so it can't regress silently.
- If a fix changes behaviour that other packages rely on (an action's payload, an error type, a config shape), update every action processor
  implementation and note it in breaking-changes.md.
- If an issue is too big to fix inline (a design problem, a cross-package change), don't half-fix it. Leave the checkbox unticked and add a note under
  the file's entry describing the issue, so it becomes its own task.

### 5. Re-run the tests

- Run the tests again after your changes. Everything should pass.
- If a test fails, work out whether the failure is expected (the test was asserting the old, broken behaviour) or a regression you introduced. Update
  the test only when the old expectation was wrong; otherwise fix the code.
- Repeat until green. Don't skip, `.todo`, or loosen a failing test to get past it.

### 6. Document the public surface

Quidproquo is a library, so anything in this file that a consumer can reach (exported from the package's index, re-exported by another package, or
part of a documented config/action surface) must be covered by the docs site at `quidproquojs.com/docusaurus/docs`.

- Work out what the file exports to users. Internal helpers that never leave the package don't need docs. Action requesters (`ask*`), config settings
  (`define*`), exported types, enums, and utilities do.
- Check whether a doc page already exists. The layout mirrors the code: action docs live under `docs/actions/<package>/<domain>/` (e.g.
  `docs/actions/core/file/ask-file-delete.md`), config docs under `docs/config/<package>/`. `quidproquojs.com/docusaurus/DOCUMENTATION_CHECKLIST.md`
  tracks overall coverage; update it when you add pages.
- Use the existing pages as the template. A good action page (see `ask-file-delete.md`) has: frontmatter with `title` and `description`, a
  one-paragraph summary, the action type and what it does on AWS, a realistic usage example, a Signature block, a Parameters table, a Returns section,
  and an Errors table listing every entry in the action's `*ErrorTypeEnum` with its meaning.
- Be in-depth, but never make things up. Every claim on the page (behaviour, defaults, error conditions, what happens on AWS) must come from reading
  the actual source and processors, not from guessing. If you're unsure what something does, go back to the code.
- Link related pages where a reader would want them: the storage drive or config setting a parameter refers to, sibling actions, the error-handling
  (`askCatch`) pattern, and related types. Relative markdown links, same as the existing pages.
- If the export doesn't fit any existing section (not an action, not config), create a new section rather than forcing it in: a new folder with a
  `_category_.json`, wired into `sidebars.ts` if needed. Keep sections and pages in a sensible order, alphabetical within a domain unless the existing
  folder uses another order.
- If the code changed in step 4 in a way that affects documented behaviour, update the existing page in the same pass so docs and code never drift.

### 7. Check it off

- With the file reviewed, issues fixed, tests passing, and its public surface documented, tick the file's checkbox below.
- If you found anything noteworthy (a fixed vulnerability, a deferred issue, a pattern that probably repeats in sibling files), leave a one-line note
  under the checkbox entry.
- Commit at sensible boundaries (a file or a small group of related files) so each change is easy to review and revert.
